title="Ore">ore</a> to make <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a>, with the right illustration displaying men working a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a>, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Tiangong_Kaiwu" title="Tiangong Kaiwu">Tiangong Kaiwu</a></i> encyclopedia, 1637.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/220px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/330px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif/440px-Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif 2x" data-file-width="1454" data-file-height="1036" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zhenghe-sailing-chart.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the known world by <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>: <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> at the top, <a href="/wiki/Ceylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a> at the upper right and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> along the bottom. Sailing directions and distances are marked using <i>zhenlu</i> (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">\xe9\x87\x9d\xe8\xb7\xaf</span>) or compass route.</div></div></div>\n<p>Compared to the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty#Technology,_science,_and_engineering" title="Song dynasty">science and technology in the Song dynasty</a>, the Ming dynasty perhaps saw fewer advancements in science and technology compared to the pace of discovery in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>. In fact, key advances in Chinese science in the late Ming were spurred by contact with Europe. In 1626 <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adam_Schall_von_Bell" title="Johann Adam Schall von Bell">Johann Adam Schall von Bell</a> wrote the first Chinese treatise on the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, the <i>Yuanjingshuo</i> (<i>Far Seeing Optic Glass</i>); in 1634 the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> acquired the telescope of the late <a href="/wiki/Johann_Schreck" title="Johann Schreck">Johann Schreck</a> (1576\xe2\x80\x931630).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444\xe2\x80\x93445_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444\xe2\x80\x93445-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric</a> model of the solar system was rejected by the Catholic missionaries in China, but <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>\'s ideas slowly trickled into China starting with the Polish Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Michael_Boym" class="mw-redirect" title="Michael Boym">Michael Boym</a> (1612\xe2\x80\x9359) in 1627, Adam Schall von Bell\'s treatise in 1640, and finally <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Edkins" title="Joseph Edkins">Joseph Edkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Wylie_(missionary)" title="Alexander Wylie (missionary)">Alex Wylie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Fryer_Thomas_Keane" title="John Fryer Thomas Keane">John Fryer</a> in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444\xe2\x80\x93447_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959444\xe2\x80\x93447-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Catholic Jesuits in China would promote <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernican</a> theory at court, yet at the same time embrace the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> system in their writing; it was not until 1865 that Catholic missionaries in China sponsored the heliocentric model as their Protestant peers did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong1963p._31,_footnote_1_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong1963p._31,_footnote_1-182">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031\xe2\x80\x9395) and <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a> (1231\xe2\x80\x931316) had laid the basis for <a href="/wiki/History_of_trigonometry" title="History of trigonometry">trigonometry</a> in China, another important work in Chinese trigonometry would not be published again until 1607 with the efforts of Xu Guangqi and Matteo Ricci.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1959110_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1959110-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Ironically, some inventions which had their origins in ancient China were reintroduced to China from Europe during the late Ming; for example, the <a href="/wiki/Field_mill_(carriage)" title="Field mill (carriage)">field mill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965255\xe2\x80\x93257_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965255\xe2\x80\x93257-184">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">Chinese calendar</a> was in need of reform since it inadequately measured the <a href="/wiki/Solar_year" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar year">solar year</a> at 365 \xc2\xbc days, giving an error of 10 min and 14 sec a year or roughly a full day every 128 years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Although the Ming had adopted <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a>\'s <i>Shoushi</i> calendar of 1281, which was just as accurate as the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Calendar">Gregorian Calendar</a>, the Ming Directorate of Astronomy failed to periodically readjust it; this was perhaps due to their lack of expertise since their offices had become hereditary in the Ming and the Statutes of the Ming prohibited private involvement in astronomy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> A sixth-generation descendant of the Hongxi Emperor, the "Prince" <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Zaiyu" title="Zhu Zaiyu">Zhu Zaiyu</a> (1536\xe2\x80\x931611), submitted a proposal to fix the calendar in 1595, but the ultra-conservative astronomical commission rejected it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166-185">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> This was the same Zhu Zaiyu who discovered the system of tuning known as <a href="/wiki/Equal_temperament" title="Equal temperament">equal temperament</a>, a discovery made simultaneously by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> (1548\xe2\x80\x931620) in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166\xe2\x80\x93117_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuttner1975166\xe2\x80\x93117-187">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> In addition to publishing his works on music, he was able to publish his findings on the calendar in 1597.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> A year earlier, the memorial of Xing Yunlu suggesting a calendar improvement was rejected by the Supervisor of the Astronomical Bureau due to the law banning private practice of astronomy; Xing would later serve with Xu Guangqi in reforming the calendar (Chinese: <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">\xe5\xb4\x87\xe7\xa6\x8e\xe6\x9a\xa6\xe6\x9b\xb8</span>) in 1629 according to Western standards.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878_186-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEngelfriet199878-186">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ming-marine-compass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Ming-marine-compass.jpg/220px-Ming-marine-compass.jpg" width="220" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Ming-marine-compass.jpg/330px-Ming-marine-compass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Ming-marine-compass.jpg/440px-Ming-marine-compass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="456" data-file-height="491" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ming-marine-compass.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 24-point compass chart employed by <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> during his explorations.</div></div></div>\n<p>When the Ming founder Hongwu came upon the mechanical devices housed in the Yuan dynasty\'s palace at Khanbaliq \xe2\x80\x93 such as fountains with balls dancing on their jets, <a href="/wiki/Automaton" title="Automaton">self-operating tiger automata</a>, dragon-headed devices that spouted mists of perfume, and <a href="/wiki/Striking_clock" title="Striking clock">mechanical clocks</a> in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Yi_Xing" title="Yi Xing">Yi Xing</a> (683\xe2\x80\x93727) and <a href="/wiki/Su_Song" title="Su Song">Su Song</a> (1020\xe2\x80\x931101) \xe2\x80\x93 he associated all of them with the decadence of Mongol rule and had them destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965133,_508_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965133,_508-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> This was described in full length by the Divisional Director of the Ministry of Works, Xiao Xun, who also carefully preserved details on the architecture and layout of the Yuan dynasty palace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965133,_508_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965133,_508-188">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Later, European Jesuits such as Matteo Ricci and <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Trigault" title="Nicolas Trigault">Nicolas Trigault</a> would briefly mention indigenous Chinese clockworks that featured drive wheels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965438_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965438-189">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> However, both Ricci and Trigault were quick to point out that 16th-century European clockworks were far more advanced than the common time keeping devices in China, which they listed as <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clocks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incense_clock" title="Incense clock">incense clocks</a>, and "other instruments&#160;... with wheels rotated by sand as if by water" (Chinese: <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">\xe6\xb2\x99\xe6\xbc\x8f</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965509_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965509-190">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Chinese records \xe2\x80\x93 namely the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">Yuan Shi</a></i> \xe2\x80\x93 describe the \'five-wheeled sand clock\', a mechanism pioneered by Zhan Xiyuan (<a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">fl.</a> 1360\xe2\x80\x9380) which featured the scoop wheel of Su Song\'s earlier <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_clock" title="Astronomical clock">astronomical clock</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Dial_(measurement)" title="Dial (measurement)">stationary dial face</a> over which a pointer circulated, similar to European models of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965511_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965511-191">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> This sand-driven wheel clock was improved upon by Zhou Shuxue (fl. 1530\xe2\x80\x9358) who added a fourth large gear wheel, changed gear ratios, and widened the orifice for collecting sand grains since he criticized the earlier model for clogging up too often.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965510\xe2\x80\x93511_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965510\xe2\x80\x93511-192">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ricciportrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ricciportrait.jpg/170px-Ricciportrait.jpg" width="170" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ricciportrait.jpg/255px-Ricciportrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Ricciportrait.jpg/340px-Ricciportrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="974" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ricciportrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> by Yu Wenhui, Latinized as Emmanuel Pereira, dated the year of Ricci\'s death, 1610</div></div></div>\n<p>The Chinese were intrigued with European technology, but so were visiting Europeans of Chinese technology. In 1584, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Ortelius" title="Abraham Ortelius">Abraham Ortelius</a> (1527\xe2\x80\x931598) featured in his atlas <i>Theatrum Orbis Terrarum</i> the peculiar Chinese innovation of <a href="/wiki/Wheelbarrow#Chinese_sailing_carriage" title="Wheelbarrow">mounting masts and sails onto carriages</a>, just like <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">Chinese ships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965276_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965276-193">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gonz%C3%A1lez_de_Mendoza" title="Juan Gonz\xc3\xa1lez de Mendoza">Gonzales de Mendoza</a> also mentioned this a year later \xe2\x80\x93 noting even the designs of them on Chinese silken robes \xe2\x80\x93 while <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">Gerardus Mercator</a> (1512\xe2\x80\x9394) featured them in his atlas, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> (1608\xe2\x80\x9374) in one of his famous poems, and <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Everardus_van_Braam_Houckgeest" title="Andreas Everardus van Braam Houckgeest">Andreas Everardus van Braam Houckgeest</a> (1739\xe2\x80\x931801) in the writings of his travel diary in China.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1965274\xe2\x80\x93276_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1965274\xe2\x80\x93276-194">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\nThe encyclopedist <a href="/wiki/Song_Yingxing" title="Song Yingxing">Song Yingxing</a> (1587\xe2\x80\x931666) documented a wide array of technologies, metallurgic and industrial processes in his <i><a href="/wiki/Tiangong_Kaiwu" title="Tiangong Kaiwu">Tiangong Kaiwu</a></i> encyclopedia of 1637. This includes mechanical and hydraulic powered devices for agriculture and irrigation,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong19667\xe2\x80\x9330,_84\xe2\x80\x93103_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong19667\xe2\x80\x9330,_84\xe2\x80\x93103-195">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> nautical technology such as vessel types and <a href="/wiki/Snorkeling" title="Snorkeling">snorkeling</a> gear for pearl divers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong1966171\xe2\x80\x9372,_189,_196_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong1966171\xe2\x80\x9372,_189,_196-196">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1971668_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1971668-197">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1971634,_649\xe2\x80\x9350,_668\xe2\x80\x9369_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1971634,_649\xe2\x80\x9350,_668\xe2\x80\x9369-198">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> the annual processes of <a href="/wiki/Sericulture" title="Sericulture">sericulture</a> and weaving with the <a href="/wiki/Loom" title="Loom">loom</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong196636\xe2\x80\x9336_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong196636\xe2\x80\x9336-199">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> metallurgic processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Crucible" title="Crucible">crucible</a> technique and <a href="/wiki/Quench" class="mw-redirect" title="Quench">quenching</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESong1966237,_190_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESong1966237,_190-200">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> manufacturing processes such as for roasting iron <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a> in converting sulphide to oxide in <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> used in gunpowder compositions \xe2\x80\x93 illustrating how ore was piled up with coal briquettes in an earthen furnace with a still-head that sent over sulfur as vapor that would solidify and <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallize</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987126_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987126-201">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> \xe2\x80\x93 and the use of gunpowder weapons such as a <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">naval mine</a> ignited by use of a rip-cord and <a href="/wiki/Wheellock" title="Wheellock">steel flint wheel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987205,_339ff_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987205,_339ff-202">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Cannon.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinese_Cannon.JPG/170px-Chinese_Cannon.JPG" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinese_Cannon.JPG/255px-Chinese_Cannon.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Chinese_Cannon.JPG/340px-Chinese_Cannon.JPG 2x" data-file-width="693" data-file-height="930" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Cannon.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Huolongjing" title="Huolongjing">Huolongjing</a></i>, compiled by <a href="/wiki/Jiao_Yu" title="Jiao Yu">Jiao Yu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bowen" title="Liu Bowen">Liu Bowen</a> before the latter\'s death in 1375.</div></div></div>\n<p>Focusing on agriculture in his <i>Nongzheng Quanshu</i>, the agronomist <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">Xu Guangqi</a> (1562\xe2\x80\x931633) took an interest in irrigation, fertilizers, famine relief, economic and textile crops, and empirical observation of the elements that gave insight into early understandings of chemistry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198465\xe2\x80\x9366_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198465\xe2\x80\x9366-203">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were many advances and new designs in gunpowder weapons during the beginning of the dynasty, but by the mid to late Ming the Chinese began to frequently employ European-style artillery and firearms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987372_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987372-204">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Huolongjing" title="Huolongjing">Huolongjing</a></i>, compiled by <a href="/wiki/Jiao_Yu" title="Jiao Yu">Jiao Yu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bowen" title="Liu Bowen">Liu Bowen</a> sometime before the latter\'s death on 16 May 1375 (with a preface added by Jiao in 1412),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham198724\xe2\x80\x9325_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198724\xe2\x80\x9325-205">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> featured many types of cutting-edge gunpowder weaponry for the time. This includes hollow, gunpowder-filled <a href="/wiki/Round_shot" title="Round shot">exploding cannonballs</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987264_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987264-206">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Land_mine" title="Land mine">land mines</a> that used a complex trigger mechanism of falling weights, pins, and a steel wheellock to ignite the train of fuses,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987203\xe2\x80\x93205_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987203\xe2\x80\x93205-207">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> naval mines,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987205_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987205-208">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> fin-mounted winged rockets for <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerodynamic">aerodynamic</a> control,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987498\xe2\x80\x93502_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987498\xe2\x80\x93502-209">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Multistage_rocket" title="Multistage rocket">multistage rockets</a> propelled by <a href="/wiki/Booster_rocket" class="mw-redirect" title="Booster rocket">booster rockets</a> before igniting a swarm of smaller rockets issuing forth from the end of the missile (shaped like a dragon\'s head),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987508_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987508-210">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hand_cannon" title="Hand cannon">hand cannons</a> that had up to <a href="/wiki/Gun_barrel" title="Gun barrel">ten barrels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1987229_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1987229-211">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Li_Shizhen" title="Li Shizhen">Li Shizhen</a> (1518\xe2\x80\x9393) \xe2\x80\x93 one of the most renowned <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacologists</a> and physicians <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">in Chinese history</a> \xe2\x80\x93 belonged to the late Ming period. His <i><a href="/wiki/Bencao_Gangmu" class="mw-redirect" title="Bencao Gangmu">Bencao Gangmu</a></i> is a medical text with 1,892 entries, each entry with its own name called a <i>gang</i>. The <i>mu</i> in the title refers to the synonyms of each name.<sup id="cite_ref-Yaniv_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yaniv-212">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> Inoculation, although it can be traced to earlier Chinese folk medicine, was detailed in Chinese texts by the sixteenth century. Throughout the Ming dynasty, around fifty texts were published on the treatment of smallpox.<sup id="cite_ref-Hopkins_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hopkins-213">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> In regards to <a href="/wiki/Oral_hygiene" title="Oral hygiene">oral hygiene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a> had a primitive toothbrush of a twig frayed at the end, but the Chinese were the first to invent the modern <a href="/wiki/Toothbrush" title="Toothbrush">bristle toothbrush</a> in 1498, although it used stiff pig hair.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chen_Hongshou,_Appreciating_Plums,_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Chen_Hongshou%2C_Appreciating_Plums%2C_detail.jpg/170px-Chen_Hongshou%2C_Appreciating_Plums%2C_detail.jpg" width="170" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Chen_Hongshou%2C_Appreciating_Plums%2C_detail.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="234" data-file-height="343" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chen_Hongshou,_Appreciating_Plums,_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Appreciating Plums</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Chen_Hongshou" title="Chen Hongshou">Chen Hongshou</a> (1598\xe2\x80\x931652) showing a lady holding an oval fan while enjoying the beauty of the plum.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sinology" title="Sinology">Sinologist historians</a> debate the population figures for each era in the Ming dynasty. The historian <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Brook" title="Timothy Brook">Timothy Brook</a> notes that the Ming government census figures are dubious since fiscal obligations prompted many families to underreport the number of people in their households and many county officials to underreport the number of households in their jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199827_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199827-215">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> Children were often underreported, especially female children, as shown by skewed population statistics throughout the Ming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998267_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998267-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Even adult women were underreported;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199897\xe2\x80\x9399_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199897\xe2\x80\x9399-217">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> for example, the Daming Prefecture in <a href="/wiki/North_Zhili" title="North Zhili">North Zhili</a> reported a population of 378,167 males and 226,982 females in 1502.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199897_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199897-218">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> The government attempted to revise the census figures using estimates of the expected average number of people in each household, but this did not solve the widespread problem of tax registration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199828,_267_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199828,_267-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Some part of the gender imbalance may be attributed to the practice of female <a href="/wiki/Infanticide" title="Infanticide">infanticide</a>. The practice is well documented in China, going back over two thousand years, and it was described as "rampant" and "practiced by almost every family" by contemporary authors.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> However, the dramatically skewed sex ratios, which many counties reported exceeding 2:1 by 1586, cannot likely be explained by infanticide alone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199897\xe2\x80\x9399_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199897\xe2\x80\x9399-217">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/170px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/255px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/340px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2022" data-file-height="2582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> (r. 1425\xe2\x80\x9335); he stated in 1428 that his populace was dwindling due to palace construction and military adventures. But the population was rising under him, a fact noted by Zhou Chen \xe2\x80\x93 governor of <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">South Zhili</a> \xe2\x80\x93 in his 1432 report to the throne about widespread itinerant commerce.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199828_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199828-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The number of people counted in the census of 1381 was 59,873,305; however, this number dropped significantly when the government found that some 3 million people were missing from the tax census of 1391.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199827\xe2\x80\x9328_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199827\xe2\x80\x9328-222">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Even though underreporting figures was made a capital crime in 1381, the need for survival pushed many to abandon the tax registration and wander from their region, where Hongwu had attempted to impose rigid immobility on the populace. The government tried to mitigate this by creating their own conservative estimate of 60,545,812 people in 1393.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199828_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199828-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> In his <i>Studies on the Population of China</i>, Ho Ping-ti suggests revising the 1393 census to 65 million people, noting that large areas of North China and frontier areas were not counted in that census.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHo19598\xe2\x80\x939,_22,_259_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHo19598\xe2\x80\x939,_22,_259-223">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Brook states that the population figures gathered in the official censuses after 1393 ranged between 51 and 62 million, while the population was in fact increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199828_221-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199828-221">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Even the <a href="/wiki/Hongzhi_Emperor" title="Hongzhi Emperor">Hongzhi Emperor</a> (r. 1487\xe2\x80\x93505) remarked that the daily increase in subjects coincided with the daily dwindling amount of registered civilians and soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199895_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199895-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> William Atwell states that around 1400 the population of China was perhaps 90 million people, citing Heijdra and Mote.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAtwell200286_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAtwell200286-224">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historians are now turning to local <a href="/wiki/Gazetteer" title="Gazetteer">gazetteers</a> of Ming China for clues that would show consistent growth in population.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998267_216-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998267-216">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Using the gazetteers, Brook estimates that the overall population under the <a href="/wiki/Chenghua_Emperor" title="Chenghua Emperor">Chenghua Emperor</a> (r. 1464\xe2\x80\x931487) was roughly 75 million,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199828,_267_219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199828,_267-219">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> despite mid-Ming census figures hovering around 62 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199895_178-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199895-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> While prefectures across the empire in the mid-Ming period were reporting either a drop in or stagnant population size, local gazetteers reported massive amounts of incoming vagrant workers with not enough good cultivated land for them to till, so that many would become drifters, conmen, or wood-cutters that contributed to deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199894\xe2\x80\x9396_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199894\xe2\x80\x9396-225">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hongzhi_Emperor" title="Hongzhi Emperor">Hongzhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zhengde_Emperor" title="Zhengde Emperor">Zhengde</a> emperors lessened the penalties against those who had fled their home region, while the <a href="/wiki/Jiajing_Emperor" title="Jiajing Emperor">Jiajing Emperor</a> (r. 1521\xe2\x80\x9367) finally had officials register migrants wherever they had moved or fled in order to bring in more revenues.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook199897_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook199897-218">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even with the Jiajing reforms to document migrant workers and merchants, by the late Ming era the government census still did not accurately reflect the enormous growth in population. Gazetteers across the empire noted this and made their own estimations of the overall population in the Ming, some guessing that it had doubled, tripled, or even grown fivefold since 1368.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998162_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998162-226">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Fairbank estimates that the population was perhaps 160 million in the late Ming dynasty,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006128_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFairbankGoldman2006128-227">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> while Brook estimates 175 million,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998162_226-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998162-226">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> and Ebrey states perhaps as large as 200 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999195_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1999195-228">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> However, a great epidemic that entered China through the northwest in 1641 ravaged the densely populated areas along the Grand Canal; a gazetteer in northern <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> noted more than half the population fell ill that year and that 90% of the local populace in one area was dead by 1642.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrook1998163_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrook1998163-229">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint Ming</a></li></ul>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Primary capital after 1403; secondary capital after 1421.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Secondary capital until 1421; primary capital afterwards.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The capitals-in-exile of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming" title="Southern Ming">Southern Ming</a> were <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming#Nanjing" title="Southern Ming">Nanjing</a> <small>(1644)</small>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming#Fuzhou" title="Southern Ming">Fuzhou</a> <small>(1645\xe2\x80\x9346)</small>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming#The_Guangzhou_court_(1646\xe2\x80\x931647)" title="Southern Ming">Guangzhou</a> <small>(1646\xe2\x80\x9347),</small> <a href="/wiki/Zhaoqing" title="Zhaoqing">Zhaoqing</a> <small>(1646\xe2\x80\x9352)</small>.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span
